Topic 525 Version 12.0

---

# Diagnosis and management of asthma in older adults

Diagnosis and management of asthma in older adults

Author:
    Carlos A Vaz Fragoso, MD
Section Editors:
    Peter J Barnes, DM, DSc, FRCP, FRS
    Kenneth E Schmader, MD
Deputy Editor:
    Helen Hollingsworth, MD

Contributor Disclosures

All topics are updated as new evidence becomes available and our peer review process is complete.

Literature review current through: Feb 2018. | This topic last updated: Dec 01, 2016.

INTRODUCTION — Asthma is common in adults over age 65 [1,2]. It substantially reduces both psychological and physical quality of life among older adults [2-5]. 

Some experts distinguish two categories of asthma in older adults. The first category comprises older patients whose typical symptoms of asthma developed in childhood or early adulthood and persisted through later life. Normal lung function is usually interspersed with intermittent periods of airflow obstruction, although chronic airway remodeling may lead to airflow limitation that does not fully reverse. The second category, which is less common, comprises patients who first develop new symptoms of asthma as older adults. The diagnosis in the first category is usually reasonably clear. The diagnosis in the second category may be challenging due to the higher incidence of COPD and the longer list of differential diagnoses. We find these two categories helpful for the discussion of the disease, but acknowledge their limitations. The clinical course may be more complex; for example, childhood asthma usually remits in adolescence, but often reappears later in adulthood. Evidence is lacking for the definition of these categories and for treatment strategies based upon these categories.

The clinical manifestations, evaluation, and management of asthma in older adults will be reviewed here. General topics on the evaluation, diagnosis, and management of asthma and COPD are discussed separately. (See "Diagnosis of asthma in adolescents and adults" and "An overview of asthma management" and "Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease: Definition, clinical manifestations, diagnosis, and staging" and "Management of stable chronic obstructive pulmonary disease".)

EPIDEMIOLOGY — The prevalence of asthma in adults age 65 and older is estimated at 4 to 8 percent [6,7]. The prevalence of asthma among all adults in the United States is estimated to be 7.7 percent [8]. A separate report estimated that over two million persons with asthma are aged ≥65 years [9]. The epidemiology of asthma in general is discussed separately. (See "Epidemiology of asthma".) 

RISK FACTORS AND TRIGGERS — Potential risk factors and triggers for asthma in older adults are similar to asthma triggers in other age groups [10-16]. A questionnaire has been developed by the NAEPP to help clinicians assess the relationship between exposure to triggers and asthma symptoms (table 1). It is worthwhile to ask older patients with asthma about triggers, because more than one-third of older patients with asthma report exercise-induced asthma symptoms; half report that animal contacts, or exposure to dusts or fumes trigger their respiratory symptoms; and over two-thirds report seasonal worsening [2,17]. Asthma risk factors and asthma triggers are discussed in greater detail separately. (See "Risk factors for asthma" and "Trigger control to enhance asthma management".)

●**Atopy** – Atopy is an important contributor to asthma in older adults. Despite the decline in prevalence of atopic symptoms, IgE levels, and positive allergen skin tests from childhood to older age [18], about one-fourth of older adults have at least one positive allergen skin test and about three-fourths of older patients with asthma are allergic to one or more common indoor allergens (eg, cockroach, cat, dog, mites) [5,19].

Older, as well as younger, adults with high levels of total IgE or specific IgE to cat or mite antigen (but not timothy grass or birch) are more likely to have bronchial hyperresponsiveness (BHR) or to develop BHR during two to three years of follow-up [20,21]. Older patients with asthma who have become sensitized to cockroach have more severe asthma and may experience a faster subsequent decline in lung function [22,23]. In Sweden and Finland, indoor dampness and mold growth are risk factors for adult-onset asthma [24,25].

●**Tobacco use** – Up to half of older people with a diagnosis of asthma are current or former smokers [2]. Cigarette smoking is the major cause of COPD and emphysema, but also increases production of IgE antibodies, bronchial responsiveness and production of inflammatory markers in the sputum in older asthmatics [26]. Tobacco smoking is not a major risk factor for development of asthma in older adults but can contribute to worsened control.

●**Irritant exposures** – Irritant exposures also increase the risk of asthma. In addition to environmental tobacco smoke, wood burning stoves and smoke from indoor use of biomass fuels (eg, wood, agricultural waste) are associated with the development of asthma in adults [11,24,27].

Among adults with asthma, about one-fourth have work-related asthma [28,29]. Asthma in older women living in Northern California is associated with occupations in art, decorating, photography, technology, health professions, food preparation, cleaning, social work, and service work [30]. Exposure to dusts, gas, vapors, fumes, or sensitizers are also associated with asthma [30]. (See "Occupational asthma: Definitions, epidemiology, causes, and risk factors".)

Increases in outdoor air pollution, especially particulate matter, have been associated with asthma exacerbations and increased hospital admissions for respiratory disorders [31-38]. In a study of the respiratory effects of air pollution in older adults, daily hospital admissions for asthma were highly associated with increases in NO2 and SO2 in the cool seasons and increases in black smoke year around [39]. Ozone levels may also contribute to the development of asthma in older adults [27].

●**Comorbid conditions** – Recognized and unrecognized comorbid conditions are common in older patients with asthma. These contribute to increased asthma morbidity because they may cause reduced adherence to asthma medication.

In an analysis of the Third National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey (NHANES III), among older patients with asthma, 20 percent had heart failure, 20 percent had a history of myocardial infarction, and 45 percent had hypertension, leaving aside overlap with COPD [40].

Obesity has been suggested as a risk factor for asthma in older women [41-43]. Obesity also appears to predict asthma among men, although the magnitude of the relationship may not be as strong.

●**Medications** – Certain medications for comorbid diseases may be a risk factor for or may exacerbate asthma [44]. Medications used to treat hypertension, coronary heart disease, glaucoma, and arthritis, include agents that can exacerbate underlying asthma (eg, topical or systemic beta blockers, aspirin, or NSAIDs) [45,46].

Discontinuing the medication may lead to improved asthma control. While the incidence of adult-onset asthma typically decreases with menopause, it is increased two-fold among women taking postmenopausal hormones (estrogen only and estrogen plus progestin) compared to women not taking postmenopausal hormones [47-49]. 

CLINICAL MANIFESTATIONS — The asthma symptoms of intermittent wheeze, cough, and chest tightness are no different in older persons than in younger persons, but older individuals are less likely to report dyspnea related to airflow limitation [50-52]. Older patients may have moderate to severe airflow limitation yet may not complain of dyspnea occurring with reported attacks of wheezing, chest tightness, or cough. This may be the result of adaptation to the long-term presence of symptoms or reflect the "paradox of well-being." The latter refers to high levels of life satisfaction in older persons and can also include lower health expectations (increasing the likelihood that symptoms are not identified as a concern, or are minimized in severity) [53].

Clues to the presence of asthma include symptoms such as nasal congestion, rhinorrhea, sneezing, and ocular itching that may indicate allergic disease and nasal congestion and anosmia in the absence of itch and sneeze that may suggest nasal polyposis. Chronic cough is another important clue to asthma in nonsmoking older patients [54]. 

Older patients with asthma seen in the outpatient setting usually have a normal physical exam of the chest, thus, a lack of expiratory wheezing, prolonged expiratory time, or hyperinflation of the chest wall does not rule-out asthma in a patient with a history of asthma-like symptoms. On the other hand, pale edematous nasal mucosa or the presence of nasal polyps increase the likelihood of asthma.

EVALUATION — The evaluation of older adults with suspected asthma is focused on pulmonary function testing. A chest radiograph is almost always included in the evaluation of new onset asthma in older adults. Laboratory studies, such as skin or serologic tests for allergy, are useful in selected patients. 

Pulmonary function testing — This section focuses on the use of pulmonary function testing in older adults; PFT is discussed in more detail separately. (See "Overview of pulmonary function testing in adults" and "Pulmonary function testing in asthma".)

Spirometry — Since older patients often have few physical signs of asthma and have a high pre-test probability of COPD, spirometry with a bronchodilator should be performed for all patients with a history of dyspnea, chronic cough, reduced exercise tolerance, or asthma-like symptoms [4,55-57]. 

The majority of older individuals are able to perform spirometry, despite concerns that frailty or decreased cognitive function would impair performance [58,59]. Spirometry is easy and reliable when performed with office spirometers that comply with the National Lung Health Education Program (NLHEP) recommendations [60]. These spirometers allow patients to stop each exhalation maneuver after only six seconds (FEV6), incorporate extensive maneuver quality checks, and provide the best reference equations for older people (NHANES III) [61]. (See "Office spirometry", section on 'Equipment'.)

Airflow limitation is traditionally defined as a ratio of forced expiratory volume in one second (FEV1)/forced vital capacity (FVC) <0.7. Some authors argue that airflow limitation should be defined as an FEV1/FVC ratio below the fifth percentile lower limit of the normal range (LLN), as the FEV1/FVC ratio declines with age and a large portion of asymptomatic older adults who have never smoked have an FEV1/FVC ratio that is less than the fixed value 0.7 [62]. When making the diagnosis of asthma in older adults, either criterion is acceptable, remembering that normal spirometry does not exclude and is quite consistent with the diagnosis of asthma. On the other hand, a definition of COPD based on a post-bronchodilator FEV1/FVC ratio <0.7 is advocated in some guidelines [57]. (See "Diagnosis of asthma in adolescents and adults", section on 'Diagnosis'.)

Once the presence of airflow limitation has been identified by a reduced FEV1/FVC ratio, the FEV1 percent predicted is used to determine the severity of airflow limitation. In the USA, the National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey (NHANES) III reference equations are recommended for interpretation of spirometry results [63]. (See "Office spirometry", section on 'Interpretation'.) 

Airflow limitation may be caused by asthma, COPD, bronchiolitis obliterans, and central airway obstruction, thus further assessment is needed to confirm that the patient has asthma. Furthermore, the absence of airflow limitation does not rule out asthma, especially if the patient is not symptomatic at the time of testing, since airflow limitation in asthma is intermittent, by definition. (See "Diagnosis of asthma in adolescents and adults", section on 'Pulmonary function testing'.)

Bronchodilator response — It is helpful to assess bronchodilator reversibility in adult patients with airflow limitation on spirometry, as recommended by the NAEPP guidelines [64]. In patients with asthma, older age does not alter the acute response to inhaled bronchodilator (BD) drugs [65,66]. Patients must omit inhaled bronchodilators prior to testing, usually for a minimum of 12 hours. The average time to peak effect of a short-acting beta-agonist such as albuterol is approximately 10 minutes, which is similar in both young and older patients with asthma. The combination of albuterol and ipratropium bromide (eg, Combivent) for diagnostic testing will often produce more effective bronchodilation in older adults, but the peak effect takes 30 minutes to occur and the results are less specific for asthma [67]. In either case, the probability of asthma is increased if the post-bronchodilator FEV1 increases more than 200 mL and the increase is greater than 12 percent. If the post-bronchodilator FEV1 and/or ratio improve from obstruction into the normal range, then the probability of asthma is increased and COPD is ruled out. (See "Overview of pulmonary function testing in adults", section on 'Post-bronchodilator'.)

Some spirometers report the change in the forced expiratory flow between 25 and 75 percent of FVC (also known as the mid-maximal flow rate or FEF25-75) following inhaled bronchodilator. However, this parameter should not be used to determine a bronchodilator response, as abnormalities in these mid-range flow measurements are not specific for small airways disease and may be misleading [63].

Bronchoprovocation testing — Bronchoprovocation testing, such as a methacholine challenge test (MCT), can be helpful in the differential diagnosis of dyspnea and cough in older patients with normal spirometry, who are not taking asthma medications [50,66,68]. With normal baseline spirometry, side effects from MCTs are rare, even in older persons. 

A low provocative challenge dose (PC-20) from the MCT (<4 mg/mL) increases the probability of asthma in patients with asthma-like symptoms. The prevalence of bronchial hyperresponsiveness (BHR) is higher in older compared with middle-aged adults, even after correcting for the baseline degree of airways obstruction, smoking status, and atopy [50]. Therefore, a PC-20 threshold of <4 mg/mL to define BHR in older patients may be more appropriate than the traditional <8 mg/mL. (See "Bronchoprovocation testing", section on 'Methacholine'.)

Home peak flow monitoring — In general, lung function lability, as assessed by home peak flow monitoring twice daily, is modestly associated with asthma and BHR. However, the compliance and quality of home measurements of lung function in older persons is often suboptimal. Morning dips of 30 percent or more in PEF or FEV1 are abnormal in older people, and may help to diagnose asthma in a few patients, but high PEF lability predicts asthma poorly [69-71]. Therefore, unless the patient is highly motivated, spirometry with bronchodilator reversibility and MCT are more reliable tests for asthma than home monitoring. (See "Peak expiratory flow rate monitoring in asthma".)

Assessment of atopy — Among patients with symptoms of exertional dyspnea, cough, or wheeze, the presence of atopy makes asthma more likely than COPD [72]. In one study, the rate of atopy was 37 percent among asthmatics and 8 percent among COPD patients [72]. Although the atopy rate of the general population declines from middle-age (30 percent) to older-age (8 percent) [18], about three-fourths of older patients with asthma have a positive allergen skin test to aeroallergens, and about one-fourth of those with a cat or dog become sensitized to their pet [5]. Half of older patients with asthma living in the inner city in one study were sensitized to cockroach antigen, and this sensitivity was associated with more severe airways obstruction and hyperinflation [22]. In addition, knowledge of the allergens to which a patient is sensitized can help guide mitigation efforts. 

We suggest assessing older adults with moderately severe to severe asthma for sensitivity to indoor allergens by one of the methods described below. (See "Overview of skin testing for allergic disease".)

Allergen skin testing — Allergen skin testing is the most rapid, sensitive, and cost effective testing modality for identifying the allergens to which a patient is sensitized [64,73]. However, allergen skin testing in the older patient should avoid atrophic or sun-damaged skin [73]. The technique of allergy skin testing, selection of allergens, and interpretation are discussed separately. (See "Overview of skin testing for allergic disease".)

Serologic testing — Serologic testing for IgE-mediated allergen sensitivity is typically used in patients who are unable to undergo skin testing or when skin testing is not available, as skin prick testing is more sensitive, although this has not been specifically confirmed in older adults. In a small study, sera of older adults with asthma were more likely than patients without asthma to have a higher total IgE level and an elevated immunoassay titer to the dust mite, Dermatophagoides pteronyssinus [74]. (See "Overview of in vitro allergy tests".)

Imaging — The chest radiograph is almost always normal in patients with asthma. However, a chest radiograph is indicated when evaluating older adults for symptoms suggestive of new onset asthma to exclude alternative diagnoses. (See "Diagnosis of asthma in adolescents and adults", section on 'Imaging'.) 

Computed tomography (CT) of the chest may show bronchial airway wall thickening, airway lumen narrowing, and low attenuation areas due to hyperinflation on inspiratory scans and gas trapping on expiratory scans [75,76]. However, chest CT is rarely indicated for the evaluation of new onset asthma in older adults.

DIAGNOSIS — Confirmation of the diagnosis of asthma in older adults is based on the presence of respiratory symptoms suggestive of asthma and the demonstration of variable expiratory airflow obstruction on pulmonary function testing or peak flow monitoring in the absence of competing diagnoses. Variability in airflow obstruction may be apparent from measurements made at different points in time, in response to an asthma trigger, or after asthma therapy. As an example, a patient's FEV1 might vary from one visit to the next, before and after inhalation of methacholine, or before and after inhaled bronchodilator. The NAEPP guidelines recommend spirometry pre and post bronchodilator. (See "Diagnosis of asthma in adolescents and adults" and "Pulmonary function testing in asthma".)

DIFFERENTIAL DIAGNOSIS — The differential diagnosis of intermittent respiratory symptoms in older adults in many cases is more difficult than in younger adults due to the higher prevalence of non-asthma conditions among older patients with dyspnea. A list of causes of chronic respiratory symptoms in older adults is provided in the table (table 2).

The three most common causes of chronic or intermittent dyspnea in older adults are COPD and emphysema, heart failure (HF), and asthma [77]. The differential diagnosis can be challenging since these diseases have many symptoms in common such as intermittent dyspnea, cough, wheezing, chest tightness, nocturnal symptoms, and worsening with exercise. (See "Approach to the patient with dyspnea", section on 'Initial testing in chronic dyspnea'.) 

COPD and emphysema versus asthma — Differentiating COPD and emphysema from asthma can be difficult and COPD and asthma may co-exist in some patients. The pretest probability of COPD and emphysema in a patient with a substantial (particularly past) smoking history is much higher than for asthma. Airflow limitation that is significantly or completely reversible with inhaled bronchodilator favors a diagnosis of asthma, although many patients with COPD have significant reversibility to inhaled bronchodilators and patients with emphysema by CT scan may have complete reversibility and/or no airflow limitation. In current or former smokers with airflow limitation but without a large bronchodilator response, a low diffusing capacity (DLCO) will strongly suggest COPD and emphysema [78,79]. For patients with a reduced DLCO or hyperinflation on their chest radiograph, a chest CT scan may be considered, as emphysema is not uncommon among older patients with a clinical diagnosis of asthma [75]. (See "Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease: Definition, clinical manifestations, diagnosis, and staging".)

Heart failure versus asthma — The differentiation of HF from asthma in the ambulatory setting may be challenging, particularly when the disease is mild. Objective evidence of elevated jugular venous pressure, S3, peripheral edema, and vascular congestion on chest radiograph all increase the probability of HF. Systolic dysfunction on echocardiography makes the diagnosis of HF; signs of diastolic dysfunction (eg, increased E/A ratio) on echocardiography are less definitive as they can be positive in states of left ventricular underfilling due to low preload pressure, for example from COPD and emphysema [80], among other diagnoses. Evidence of airflow obstruction on spirometry defines obstructive airways disease. (See 'Spirometry' above.) Cardiovascular disease in ambulatory older persons usually causes only a mild reduction in the FEV1 and FVC of approximately 0.1 to 0.3 liters [81]. Substantial airflow limitation in a patient presenting to the emergency department or in the hospital is much more likely due to asthma or COPD than HF [82]. (See "Determining the etiology and severity of heart failure or cardiomyopathy".)

In patients with acute dyspnea, chest x-ray and measurement of B-type natriuretic peptide (BNP) may help differentiate these entities [83,84]. (See "Evaluation of the adult with dyspnea in the emergency department", section on 'Plain chest radiograph (CXR)' and "Evaluation of the adult with dyspnea in the emergency department", section on 'Brain natriuretic peptide'.)

Other diseases in the differential diagnosis of asthma in adults, such as central airway obstruction (eg, due to tracheal compression or obstructing tumors), bronchiectasis, and cough induced by angiotensin converting enzyme inhibitors, are discussed separately. (See "Diagnosis of asthma in adolescents and adults", section on 'Differential diagnosis'.)

GENERAL TREATMENT PRINCIPLES — Successful management of asthma in older adults is based on the same four essential components (monitoring, education, control of environmental factors, and pharmacologic therapy) that are used for management of asthma at all ages. The National Asthma Education and Prevention Program (NAEPP) and the Global Initiative for Asthma (GINA) recommend starting with a determination of asthma severity to guide initial therapy (table 3) and adjusting therapy based on subsequent assessment of asthma control using a stepwise approach, (figure 1 and table 4) [64,85]. 

The comments below outline considerations that specifically pertain to older adults; a general approach to asthma management is provided separately. (See "An overview of asthma management".)

Patient education — The three most important components of asthma education are communicating the features and mechanism of asthma, the appropriate role and use of asthma medications, and measures to treat and prevent symptoms. As with younger patients, a written asthma action plan is important to achieve optimal care [86-88]. (See "What do patients need to know about their asthma?".)

Inhaler technique — Correct inhaler technique is essential for optimal efficacy of inhaled medications for asthma. Observation of the patient’s technique is a key step in ensuring that the patient is using proper technique at home. Some older patients have difficulty learning correct inhaler technique; an abnormal Mini-Mental State Examination predicts difficulty learning and retaining inhaler technique [89-92]. (See "Evaluation of cognitive impairment and dementia".) 

Use of an HFA pressurized metered dose (MDI) device with a valved holding chamber, a breath-actuated dry powder inhaler, or a nebulizer may improve medication delivery when technique is suboptimal [93,94]. (See "Delivery of inhaled medication in adults" and "The use of inhaler devices in adults".)

Trigger mitigation — Although trials of allergen mitigation (efforts to reduce indoor allergen levels) that include older adults are lacking, mitigation is helpful in children (table 5). Environmental modifications based on allergen skin test results are suggested for older adults with asthma [95]. (See "Trigger control to enhance asthma management".)

Cigarette smoke is an airway irritant, and smoking cessation is associated with improvement in lung function, in addition to its benefits in reducing fatal and nonfatal cardiovascular disease [96]. (See "Trigger control to enhance asthma management", section on 'Irritants (including cigarette smoke)' and "Overview of smoking cessation management in adults" and "Pharmacotherapy for smoking cessation in adults".)

Vaccination — Both influenza and pneumococcal vaccines reduce respiratory infections in asthmatics [97,98]. In a retrospective review, pneumococcal vaccination was associated with a decrease in asthma admissions in older adult patients [99]. (See "Seasonal influenza vaccination in adults", section on 'Older adults' and "Pneumococcal vaccination in adults".)

Geriatric assessment — Older persons often require a comprehensive geriatric assessment, given their high likelihood of having a complex clinical presentation [100]. In particular, advancing age is associated with increased multimorbidity (62 percent of older Americans have two or more chronic conditions), medication-related adverse effects (20 to 30 percent of older Americans use medications listed in drugs-to-avoid lists), psychosocial factors (less education, sedentary lifestyle, social isolation), geriatric health conditions (cognitive and sensory impairments, falls, incontinence, weight loss, dizziness, etc), and reduced symptom awareness [50-53,100-106]. Hence, among older persons, careful consideration should be given as to whether changes in asthma status result from multifactorial mechanisms or, conversely, whether changes in clinical status result from an under-recognized asthma exacerbation.

INITIATING PHARMACOLOGIC THERAPY — A stepwise approach to asthma therapy is recommended by national and international guidelines. The goal is to control symptoms quickly and adjust therapy in a stepwise fashion as symptoms and pulmonary function require (figure 1) [64,85]. All patients should have access to a “quick-relief” short-acting beta-agonist. In addition, patients with features of mild persistent, moderate persistent, or severe asthma are also candidates for a “controller medication”, of which inhaled glucocorticoids are the preferred agent. (See "An overview of asthma management", section on 'Pharmacologic treatment'.)

Short-acting beta agonists — Inhaled short-acting inhaled beta-2-selective adrenergic agonist (SABA) therapy is recommended as needed for relief of acute asthma symptoms in older adults, similar to younger adults and adolescents (table 6) [64,85]. SABAs can increase heart rate and reduce serum potassium concentration. However, it is reassuring that among 12,090 patients age 55 or older with COPD, SABAs were not associated with an increase in the risk of fatal or nonfatal myocardial infarction [107]. We suggest that these agents be used on an as-needed basis only, as scheduled use of these agents does not confer additional benefit. (See "Treatment of moderate persistent asthma in adolescents and adults", section on 'Short-acting beta agonists'.)

Inhaled glucocorticoids — Inhaled glucocorticoids (inhaled GC) are the preferred initial controller therapy for older adults with mild persistent, moderate persistent, or severe asthma (eg, symptoms >2 days/week, nocturnal symptoms 3 to 4 times/month) and their use is associated with reduced mortality and hospitalization in older adults with asthma [108]. In contrast, for patients with COPD, inhaled GC are not first line therapy, particularly as monotherapy [109]. Selection of the initial dose of inhaled GC in asthma should follow the NAEPP guidelines (table 3 and figure 1 and table 7) [64]. (See "An overview of asthma management".)

High daily doses of inhaled GC may decrease bone mineral density and increase the risk of fracture, although data are conflicting [110-112]. Older adults on chronic therapy with inhaled GC who have a moderate risk of osteoporosis should undergo bone density measurement to assess the need for preventive therapy, as is recommended for patients receiving inhaled GC for COPD. (See "Screening for osteoporosis" and "Prevention and treatment of glucocorticoid-induced osteoporosis", section on 'General measures'.)

In addition to effects on bone density, inhaled GCs are a common cause of oral candidiasis and hoarseness in older adults, which can be mitigated by careful oral rinsing after use of the inhaled GC. For hoarseness, sometime use of a smaller particle size agent, such as beclomethasone HFA rather than a fluticasone dry powder inhaler, can reduce laryngeal deposition and dysphonia. (See "Major side effects of inhaled glucocorticoids".)

Systemic glucocorticoids — In the management of asthma, oral prednisone is generally limited to short-term "bursts", to treat an exacerbation. The usual indications for systemic glucocorticoids are a moderate (peak expiratory flow <70 percent of baseline) or severe exacerbation (peak expiratory flow <40 percent of baseline) despite a dose or two of inhaled SABA (algorithm 1 and algorithm 2). (See "Management of acute exacerbations of asthma in adults", section on 'Systemic glucocorticoids'.) 

Chronic prednisone therapy should be used only as a last resort, after the clinician has helped the patient identify and minimize exacerbating factors (environmental, drug, GERD, and rhinosinusitis), prescribed a moderate to high daily dose of inhaled GC for at least three months, tried a long-acting beta-agonist and/or a leukotriene modifier, and examined the patient closely for comorbidities that might be worsened by glucocorticoid therapy.

Chronic prednisone therapy has multiple potential side effects in older patients, especially older women, including hip and vertebral fractures, cataracts, poor glycemic control, and herpes zoster [110]. (See "Major side effects of systemic glucocorticoids" and "Screening for osteoporosis" and "Prevention and treatment of glucocorticoid-induced osteoporosis".)

ADJUSTING CONTROLLER THERAPY — Asthma therapy is adjusted based upon an assessment of asthma control and any adverse effects of medication, remembering that the degree of asthma control is determined by the most severe indicator of impairment (table 4). Assessment of asthma control is described separately. (See "An overview of asthma management", section on 'Adjusting medication in patients already on controller therapy'.)

When asthma is not well-controlled on low-to-medium dose inhaled GC, possible causes of poor adherence should first be considered, such as the cost of the inhalers, difficulty using the inhaler, depression, or worries about steroid side effects [113-115]. Occult smoking, which decreases the effectiveness of ICS therapy, may also be playing a role.

When choosing whether to increase the dose of inhaled GC, add a long-acting beta agonist (LABA), or add an antileukotriene agent, we assess patient preferences, co-morbidities, and likelihood of success. As examples:

●For patients whose asthma is not controlled by a low initial dose of inhaled GC, we typically increase the dose of inhaled GC, although adding a LABA is a reasonable alternative (figure 1).

●In the absence of cardiovascular disease, we suggest adding a LABA in patients who are inadequately controlled on medium to high dose inhaled GC or do not tolerate higher doses of inhaled GC. Leukotriene receptor antagonists are an alternative. (See 'Inhaled glucocorticoids' above and 'Long-acting beta agonists' below and 'Anti-leukotriene agents' below.)

●For patients with known cardiovascular disease (eg, atrial fibrillation or coronary heart disease), a**** higher dose of inhaled GC or an antileukotriene agent may be preferable to a LABA, although data regarding LABA safety in older adults are generally favorable. 

●For patients at high risk for GC side effects (eg, hoarseness, osteoporosis, or glaucoma), it may be preferable to add a LABA or antileukotriene agent, so as to maintain a lower dose of inhaled GC. 

Patients whose asthma has been well-controlled over a period of three to six months on a stable medication regimen can have their therapy stepped down. This process is described separately. (See "Treatment of moderate persistent asthma in adolescents and adults", section on 'Decreasing therapy'.)

Long-acting beta agonists — According to the NAEPP and GINA guidelines, long-acting beta-agonist (LABA) therapy with salmeterol or formoterol is indicated in patients whose asthma is not well-controlled on inhaled GC alone (figure 1 and table 8) [64,85]. LABAs are used in combination with another controller agent, such as inhaled GCs, and are not used as monotherapy, due to concerns that LABAs may be associated with rare severe asthma exacerbations and increased asthma and cardiac mortality in a small subgroup of patients. (See "Beta agonists in asthma: Controversy regarding chronic use", section on 'Long-acting beta agonists'.)

The safety of salmeterol in older adults with asthma has not been directly assessed in large groups of patients [116]. The most reassuring data regarding cardiovascular safety of LABAs comes from studies of older adults with COPD. As an example, patients with COPD experience no greater morbidity when using salmeterol with fluticasone compared with fluticasone alone [109]. Generally, in older adults with asthma, the dose should be limited to 50 mcg twice daily for salmeterol and 12 mcg twice daily for formoterol, in order to limit the risk of cardiovascular toxicity (table 6). (See "Management of the patient with COPD and cardiovascular disease", section on 'Long-acting beta agonists' and "Management of the patient with COPD and cardiovascular disease", section on 'Combination inhaled bronchodilator plus glucocorticoid'.)

Anti-leukotriene agents — Addition of an antileukotriene agent (table 9) is a reasonable addition to a medium dose of inhaled GC in older patients, rather than increasing inhaled GC therapy to high daily doses. In one large four week, open-label trial of leukotriene antagonist therapy, asthma symptoms improved in 54 percent of older patients with mild asthma, 63 percent with moderate asthma, and 70 percent with severe asthma [117]. Improvements in pulmonary function testing and reduction in beta-agonist use were not as great among older adult as adolescent and adult patients with asthma. (See "Antileukotriene agents in the management of asthma".)

Anti-IgE therapy — Anti-IgE therapy (omalizumab) is approved for use in patients with moderate to severe persistent asthma inadequately controlled with inhaled glucocorticoids, with a total serum IgE level between 30 and 700 international units/mL, and with allergic sensitization demonstrated by positive skin testing or in vitro testing for allergen-specific IgE to an allergen that is present year round (a perennial allergen), such as dust mite, animal danders, cockroach, or molds. (See "Anti-IgE therapy".)

Only a few studies have examined the efficacy in older adults, but it appears comparable to that in younger patients. In a pooled analysis of randomized trial data, the responses of subjects age 50 and older to omalizumab were compared with placebo [118]. Omalizumab reduced the risk of clinically significant asthma exacerbations, improved asthma symptom scores, and reduced rescue medication use. Another study found that patients age 50 years or older, compared with younger patients, experienced a comparable reduction in exacerbation rate (69 versus 75 percent), slightly less of an improvement in asthma symptoms and nocturnal awakenings (68 and 73 percent in the older versus 79 and 82 percent in the younger patients), and a similar improvement in lung function [119]. There was a higher rate of discontinuation of omalizumab therapy in older patients (21 vs 11 percent). The adverse effects associated with omalizumab have not been specifically addressed in older adults, but include anaphylaxis, urticaria, and other hypersensitivity reactions. A possible association with cardiovascular and cerebrovascular disease has been suggested by interim data from a post-marketing observational study, but is not considered conclusive by the FDA. These effects are discussed separately. (See "Anti-IgE therapy", section on 'Adverse effects'.)

Anticholinergic agents — Short-acting anticholinergic therapy with ipratropium has not been proven to be effective as a controller agent for asthma [120,121]. While not standard therapy for asthma according to the NAEPP guidelines [64], adding the long-acting anticholinergic agent tiotropium to inhaled glucocorticoids may be beneficial in patients whose asthma is not controlled with inhaled glucocorticoids plus a LABA. Potential adverse effects include dry mouth and urinary retention. The role of tiotropium in the management of severe asthma and its potential adverse effects are discussed separately. (See "Treatment of severe asthma in adolescents and adults", section on 'Tiotropium' and "Role of anticholinergic therapy in COPD", section on 'Potential risks'.) 

Theophylline — Theophylline is not suggested for the routine treatment of asthma in older adults. Chronic overmedication with theophylline remains common and causes major toxic events [122]. In addition, a number of medication interactions can result in toxicity. (See "Theophylline poisoning" and "Theophylline use in asthma", section on 'Factors affecting metabolism'.)

PROGNOSIS — Adult onset asthma is more likely to become life-long when compared to asthma beginning in childhood and to have greater morbidity [10,123]. Compared with younger adults with asthma, older adults have more hospitalizations, more comorbidities, and poorer lung function [123-126]. In a large United States database examined from 2006 to 2008, older adults with asthma had a 5-fold increased risk of overall mortality compared with younger adults with asthma even after adjustment for comorbidities [126]. However, a six-year observational study of 4756 patients with asthma, The Epidemiology and Natural History of Asthma: Outcomes and Treatment Regimens (TENOR) study, reported that the hospitalization rate was actually lower among the cohort age 65 and older than the younger cohort [86]. The explanation for the lower hospitalization rate may have been more aggressive asthma care among the older adults in this supervised population, suggesting that optimizing asthma care has the potential to improve outcomes.

SOCIETY GUIDELINE LINKS — Links to society and government-sponsored guidelines from selected countries and regions around the world are provided separately. (See "Society guideline links: Asthma in adults".)

SUMMARY AND RECOMMENDATIONS

●Asthma is common in adults over age 65. Some patients will have had asthma since childhood and others may have developed asthma as adults. Asthma is less likely to resolve spontaneously in older adults. (See 'Epidemiology' above.)

●Older patients with asthma are just as likely to report cough, wheeze and chest tightness as younger patients. However, older patients are less likely to report dyspnea at a given degree of airflow limitation. (See 'Clinical manifestations' above.)

●Although older adults with asthma are less likely to be atopic than younger adults, atopy is still common and three-fourths will have a positive skin test to at least one indoor allergen. (See 'Risk factors and triggers' above and 'Allergen skin testing' above.)

●Identifying and avoiding asthma triggers (eg, smoke or smoking, animal dander, occupational agents) often helps control asthma symptoms (table 1 and table 5). (See "Trigger control to enhance asthma management".)

●Spirometry is accurate in most older adults and an important part of the diagnosis and management of asthma. (See 'Spirometry' above and "Diagnosis of asthma in adolescents and adults", section on 'Diagnosis'.)

●Tests of bronchodilator and methacholine responsiveness are accurate in most older adults and help in the diagnosis of asthma. Tests of diffusing capacity, measurement of bronchodilator response, and chest computed tomography, in selected patients, help to differentiate asthma from COPD and emphysema. (See 'Pulmonary function testing' above and 'COPD and emphysema versus asthma' above.) 

●We recommend that acute asthma symptoms in older adults be treated with a short-acting beta agonist, such as albuterol (Grade 1A). (See 'Short-acting beta agonists' above.)

●In patients with mild persistent asthma, we suggest treatment with controller therapy (Grade 2B). In patients with asthma more severe than this, we recommend controller therapy (table 3 and figure 1) (Grade 1A). The preferred initial controller therapy is inhaled glucocorticoid (GC); selection of the initial dose is based on symptom frequency and severity and degree of airflow obstruction (figure 1 and table 7). (See 'Inhaled glucocorticoids' above.)

●At follow-up visits, when asthma is not well-controlled or is poorly controlled (table 4), asthma therapy is increased in a stepwise fashion as described in the figure (figure 1). When choosing whether to increase the dose of inhaled GC, add a long-acting beta agonist (LABA), tiotropium, or an antileukotriene agent, we assess patient preferences, co-morbidities, and likelihood of success. As examples:

•For patients whose asthma is not controlled by a low initial dose of inhaled GC, we typically increase the dose of inhaled GC, although adding a LABA is a reasonable alternative. (See 'Adjusting controller therapy' above.)

•In the absence of cardiovascular disease, we suggest adding a LABA in patients who are inadequately controlled on medium to high dose inhaled GC or do not tolerate higher doses of inhaled GC (Grade 2B). Leukotriene receptor antagonists and tiotropium are an alternative. (See 'Inhaled glucocorticoids' above and 'Long-acting beta agonists' above and 'Anti-leukotriene agents' above.)

•For patients with known cardiovascular disease, a**** higher dose of inhaled GC or an antileukotriene agent may be preferable to a LABA or tiotropium, although data regarding LABA and tiotropium safety in older adults are generally favorable. (See 'Adjusting controller therapy' above.)

•For patients at high risk for GC side effects (eg, hoarseness, osteoporosis, or glaucoma), it may be preferable to add a LABA, tiotropium, or antileukotriene agent, so as to maintain a lower dose of inhaled GC. (See 'Adjusting controller therapy' above.)

●Although data are limited, anti-IgE therapy (omalizumab) appears to have comparable efficacy in older adults with moderate to severe asthma who meet the usual guidelines. (See 'Anti-IgE therapy' above and "Anti-IgE therapy", section on 'Indications and patient selection'.)

●Theophylline is not generally indicated to treat asthma in older adults due to the increased risk of toxicity. (See 'Theophylline' above.)

●Older persons with a chronic condition often require a comprehensive geriatric assessment and treatment plan, given the likelihood of having a complex clinical presentation.

ACKNOWLEDGMENTS — The editorial staff at UpToDate would like to acknowledge Paul Enright, MD, and R Graham Barr, MD, DrPH, who contributed to an earlier version of this topic review.
